>
> B) The whole 8:9:15 "triad" really sounds more like a "tetrad" because of how widely spread out it it.  If 12EDO was allowed to use this spread triad "cheat"...you'd get added "triads" like C E G, C D G, C G A, B E A...and so on.  Again the apparent advantage in number of possible chords in 12TET seems downright huge.

11-EDO is tricky. I'm starting to really like it because of the
2.7.9.11 subgroup stuff, but it's tricky. However, it's one of the
low-numbered, practical EDOs that the universe gifted us with, so it's
worthwhile figuring out how to use it. Obviously 12-EDO has some
advantages, like that there's a near-perfect 3/2, and 5-limit harmony
is great, and 7 is also decent, and so on. But 11-EDO still exists, so
it's worthwhile figuring out how to use. I don't really have my head
wrapped around all of it yet. Igs sounds like he's starting to get it
and I look forward to his new composition.

As a related tangent - check out the porcupine comma pump I just
posted. To my ears, it sounds very "natural," in that it sounds like
common practice music that just so happens to be built on porcupine
instead of meantone. I used mostly standard common practice
techniques, but worked the whole thing out so that the progression
only lines up properly if you're working within a porcupine
temperament. The trick is generalizing this "functional" business to
higher-limit chords and making things want to resolve. I have no idea
how to do it if 3/2 isn't involved. Either way, once that's figured
out, working in temperaments like 11-EDO will be a lot easier.

-Mike

ðŸ”—cityoftheasleep <igliashon@...>

4/22/2011 11:56:02 PM

--- In tuning@yahoogroups.com, Michael <djtrancendance@...> wrote:
> >"The thing with 11-EDO is that you can represent large clusters of it
> > with relatively low harmonics (4:7:9:11:15:17, for instance--that's 6
> > notes, so more than half of 11-EDO right there"
>
>   Pretty cool...if you have the uncanny ability to keep track of chord spread across 2
> octaves like that.

It's not supposed to be played as a big block chord (though I guess you could...), the point was to show that melodies won't have trouble running into/being close tones with notes in the chords.

> A) That you're still really dealing, if I have it right, with two types of triads...whereas in 12TET (even in diatonic) you get major/minor/augmented/diminished.

Nonsense! The augmented and diminished are not treated as consonances in 12-TET, so I don't think it's fair to count them. If we're only counting triads that span an 8/5 or less, and not counting inversions as separate chords, 12-TET has 4:5:6, 10:12:15, 8:9:10, and 6:8:9 as its consonant triads. 11-EDO has 7:8:9, 7:9:11, 8:9:11, and [maaaaybe] 11:15:17. Just because they all come together to form a giant hexad doesn't invalidate each triad in itself; in fact, that's kind of a killer feature. But if we want to add scalar dissonances that work like augmented and diminished, we can do that, too.

For each triad, there's a scale that gives plenty of that triad. For 7:8:9, there's the hexatonic 2 2 2 2 2 1 I already mentioned. It's generated by the 2nd degree of 11-EDO, sort of a stretched whole-tone scale. We get four 7:8:9 triads, one 7:8:9:11 tetrad, and on the IV and V degrees, we get 8:9:11 triads; on the VI degree, we get a very dissonant 15:16:18 triad. Note we can invert these chords to span more than an octave, to make 4:7:9, 4:7:9:11, and 4:9:11 consonances and an 8:15:18 "diminished" chord. This scale is also the best thing going for the questionably-consonant 11:15:17 triads, giving three of them (on degrees IV, V, and VI).

For 7:9:11, we can use the 4th degree of 11-EDO as a generator, giving a 5-note and an 8-note scale, 3L+2s and 3L+5s (respectively). The latter kind of sucks melodically, but gives 7:9:11 triads on 6 degrees; I like 1 2 1 1 2 1 2 1 as the tonic mode, since it makes the VIII degree into a "diminished" chord (0-4-6) that's maybe a badly-tempered 7:9:10 (if anything), and the V degree sort of an "augmented" (0-5-7) that's our questionable 11:15:17 triad again.

For 8:9:11, we have the scale generated by the 3rd degree of 11-EDO, which has a 7-note MOS of 4L+3s. Using the 2 2 1 2 1 2 1 mode, we have otonal triads on I, II, IV, and VI, utonal triads on II, IV, VI, and VII, and diminished triads of 14:15:18 on III and V. We also get a bonus 7:8:9:11 triad on the I, among other possibilities. I might like this scale better than the octatonic one above. It's a toss-up as to which of these three makes a better "white-key" scale, but I think they're all good, and with a little consideration can be as useful as anything 12 has to offer.

The other degrees of 11 (1, 5, 6, and 10) all generate scales that are, well, kind of melodically horrendous (at least insofar as they'd make terrible white-key scales), and also are less efficient than the above three for the available consonant harmonies.

-Igs
